  • 00:00 to 34:00: Debugger: To debug is to correct errors or mistakes in the code. It's a way to improve. There are different forms to debug, but browsers has their own tools to debug in DevTools. It's common to have errors while coding. In DevTools/Sources we can access to the index.html. Clicking the column of numbers we can create breakpoints. When reload the code will pause in the Breakpoint. With F8 the code will continue running until the next breakpoint. That happens only when the DevTools are opened. Breakpoint are useful to watch what is the code doing in real time. With F10 we can pass to the next line, and with Ctrl + F8 to deactivate the breakpoint. In the right hand we have an area where we can see detailed information about the breakpoints, above it, there is the Scope. Scope allows us to know where are we, and what we have in the Global Scope, that means the object window (the object window will be always available there) and what we have available in the script, for this example two functions created by Juan. Watching in the Scope we emerge some comments about hoisting, because the variable let a = 10; appears in the Scope with value undefined before the initialization, after it, Scope shows that "a" gets the value asigned. We can change the value of a variable in DevTool/Source/Scope and the value will remain with that value. It's like to enter to the core of the code and change a value deep inside the code. The debugger is enjoyable and amusing. We also have Watch: where we can asset expressions, understud as something we can assign to a variable, like: 2: 2; 2 < 3: true; functions as getCompleteName(): "Andr칠z Guti칠rrez"; sayHello(). The following are not expressions: if, while, for.
  • 34:00 to 44:00: When passing to the next step we can press F11, if there is something to enter (like a function) it enters. In DevTool/Source we also have the Call Stack ("Pila de llamadas"). When we set a Breakpoint, Callstack shows if the point where we are in the code is anonymous (code out or a box or cage, like a free code) or belongs to a escructure with a name. Callstack represents the movements of the code and the process of reading. So it shows where the reading is awating mean something else is read in other part of the code, for example a function or a callback. Therefore each process is stacked one on top of another and the last one must be finished to also finish the one below. When we are within a function Scope shows script, global and local, the last one indicates the elements available just inside that function.
  • 44:00 to 1:26:00: We can force the debugger to be executed adding the expression debugger in any part of the code, and when DevTools are open the code will stop in this specific line. We would like to do that if we want some part or an specific part of the code to be highlighted: if (b === 0) {debugger}. Some comments about Math.random(), creates random number from 0 to 1. If we multiply it by 10, we're going to have number from 0 to 10: Math.random() * 10. And if we want to round the number we can write: Math.round(Math.random() * 10). For create random numbers, according to Juan they use the hour. In debugger we can also see events, all things related with mouse, keyboard, if we select in mouse click it will show us all the addEventListener we have created. DevTools is a big world.   • 1:27:00 to 1:54:00: Advanced events. First a summary of how to add events directly in the HTML: <button class="btn-click" onclick="console.log('Hola');">Haceme click!</button>, To see more: Class 20 - Events. That's not a proper way to do that. Now, if we want to assign a primitive value (number, boolean or string) from JavaScript to the property onClick, using querySelector we won't be able to do it, like this:
    1. const btnClick = document.querySelector('.btn-click'); // create the constant btnClick and asign the value of the selector .btn-click, that means the button.
    2. btnClick.onClick = `console.log('Hola')`; // It won't work. It's like onClick refuse to recieve data from scripts, data should be add directly within the HTML tag.
    A summary of the use of backtips and backslash \ for strings. Returning to onClick we do can add a callback, like this:
    • btnClick.onclick = function () { console.log('Hiciste click!'); }; console.log(btnClick.onclick); // console.log will show that the onClick attribute has the function.
    Also we can reassign a new callback to the event. But watch out with the following error when assigning a callback to an event: btn.onClick = one(); in this case the parenthesis execute the function, so in reality we are not assigning the function but the value returned by it. To avoid this we need to remove the parenthesis.
  • 1:55:00 to 2:04:00: Multiple callback assignation to the event click. Well we can use the addEventListener method for the event 'click':
    • btnClick.addEventListener('click', uno);
    • btnClick.addEventListener('click', dos);
    • btnClick.addEventListener('click', () => { console.log('Tres!'); });
    • btnClick.addEventListener('click', () => console.log('Cuatro!'));
    • btnClick.addEventListener('click', function () { console.log('Cinco!'); });
    In DevTools, Elements, we can select the button (btnClick element) and in the tab Event Listeners we will see all the Event Listerners available, even we can remove some of them, or all of them. Also, in Sources in the folder main.js/Event Listener Breakpointss/Mouse/Click to check and when reload the page, they will react or paused in any event available.
  • 2:05:00 to 2:11:00: Object event. When we regiter an event with addEventListener: btnClick.addEventListener('click', () => {}); inside the partenthesis we can add a variable or object. That variable will be called as we want, usually we use the name event or e. That element will give us information about the event, e.g. in what coordinates was clicked on:
    • btnClick.addEventListener('click', (e) => {console.log('e.pageX x e.pageY:', e.pageX + 'x' + e.pageY);});
    • console.log(e); // within the addEventListener scope to see the information of the object e in the console.
  • 2:11:00 to 2:15:00: The callback can be defined outside. Like this:
    • const mostrarCoordenadasMouse = e => {console.log('e.pageX x e.pageY:', e.pageX + 'x' + e.pageY);};
    • btnClick.addEventListener('click', mostrarCoordenadasMouse); // Watch out! If the function had parentheses it would be executed and the callback would not be passed.

    • const dos = document.querySelector('.box--2');
    • const tres = document.querySelector('.box--3');
    Box one contains box two and three and box two contains box three. Now we add a Listener to the event click with addEventListener:
    • tres.addEventListener('click', e => {console.log('*** CLICK EN TRES ***');});
    • dos.addEventListener('click', e => {console.log('** CLICK EN DOS **');});
    • uno.addEventListener('click', e => {console.log('* CLICK EN UNO *');});
    By default the behaviour of the event propagation when click is bubbling, that means when we click the box three also we will be interacting with box 2 and box 1, moving from the most specific to the most general

  • 00:00 to 06:00: As we see before bubbling is the default behaviour of events, going from the deepest element to the outermost.
    • tres.addEventListener('click', e => {console.log('*** CLICK EN TRES ***');});
    • dos.addEventListener('click', e => {console.log('** CLICK EN DOS **');});
    • uno.addEventListener('click', e => {console.log('* CLICK EN UNO *');});
    With bubbling the reaction occurs from the element clicked to the element container. Now, it's important to know that addEventListener has three spaces within the parenthesis: addEventListener('click', callback, boolean). The boolean is related with the propagation of the event, hence by default is false, that means bubbling, when true, means capturing. When capturing is setted the event progagation will go from outermost to the deepest , contrary to what bubbling does.

Class 37 (Alumni 38) - 9/22/2022. Regular expressions, new RegExp, object type declarative form, literal declarative form, flab 'i', method .test.

Juan's notes Class 37

  • Class - 37: Regular expressions

Timeline and notes Class 37.

  • 00:00 to 27:00 Some questions, explanation about the use of regular expressions, emails, credit cards, adresses, phone number, zip numbers. According to MDN: "Regular expressions are patterns used to match character combinations in strings. In JavaScript, regular expressions are also objects. These patterns are used with the exec() and test() methods of RegExp, and with the match(), matchAll(), replace(), replaceAll(), search(), and split() methods of String. Using a regular expression literal, which consists of a pattern enclosed between slashes, as follows: const re = /ab + c/;". We can create regular expressions in JavaScript in different forms:
    • Object type declarative form: // with new RegExp(), it has a method .test to evaluate the string.
      • const finalTexto = 'la';
      • const texto = 'h' + 'o' + finalTexto;
      • const pruebaRegExp = new RegExp(texto);
      • const pruebaRegExp = new RegExp('abc');
      • const pruebaRegExp = new RegExp('abc', 'i');
    • Literal declarative form: const pruebaRegExp = /abc/i; // This is prefered, even sonarlint recommends to use it instead of new RegExp. But new RegExp is useful when using a prompt or a variable. The letter i is a flag that means insensitive to uppercases and lowercases. Flags goes always at the end of the expression, and there are others.
    Now we can test if the strings below match with the regular expression set:
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp);
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('')); // false
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test(' ')); // false
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('hola, qu칠 tal?')); // false
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('a b c')); // false
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('abc')); // true
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('ABc')); // true
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('abC')); // true
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('-abc?')); // true
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('aBCdef')); // true
    • console.log(pruebaRegExp.test('ZZZ....ABC####zz')); // true
    There are other methods besides test for regular expressions.
  • 27:00 to 30:00:Som conditionals to test the regular expressions:
    • if (/hola/.test('hola')) {console.log('sip');} else {console.log('nop');} // true
    • if (/hola/.test('hOLa')) {console.log('sip');} else {console.log('nop');} // false
    • if (/hola/i.test('hOLa')) console.log('sip');} else {console.log('nop');} // true
    • if (/hola/i.test('chau')) console.log('sip');} else {console.log('nop');} // false
  • 30:00 to 40:00: Also, we can use a ternay:
    • const texto = 'textooxyzooooo';
    • const contieneXYZ = /xyz/i.test(texto) ? 'S칈' : 'NO';
    • console.log(contieneXYZ); // returns S칈
    That way we can evaluate some other strings:
    • console.log(/xyz/i.test('xyz')?'S칈':'NO'); // true
    • console.log(/xyz/i.test('holaxyz')?'S칈':'NO'); // true
    • console.log(/xyz/i.test('holaxyzhola')?'S칈':'NO'); // true
    • console.log(/xyz/i.test('XYZXYZhola----.asdf.asdfxyz')?'S칈':'NO'); // true

  • 33:00 to 59:00:contenteditable. It's a property of HTML that allows two values: false and true. With the value of true users can modify the element, add text and press enter creating spaces. Now, we can add the property focus to play a little bit. If we put focus in a h1 element, nothing happens, but if we put it in an element with contenteditable true it's going to work, as it works with link elements (a tag). We can add style in CSS to the focus property using the pseudoclass :focus. p:focus, for all element p with property focus and just :focus for all elements with that property. An interesting property is outline: similar to a border, but is not the same, and when active doesn't change the size of the element. Outline is the frame that appears when an element is in focus. To add style to element with attributes we can use the attribute selector. For example for all elements with the attribute [title] {font-family: Arial, "Helvetica Neue", Helvetica, sans-serif;}; and also for all elements with the attibute title and the value Hello 2 [title="Hello 2"] {font-family: Arial, "Helvetica Neue", Helvetica, sans-serif;}. We can do the same for the attribute content edittable:
    • [contenteditable]{}
    • [contenteditable="true"]{}
    • h3[contenteditable="true"]{} // without space.
    Also it's possible to add the property contenteditable to a div, with list elements, and the user will be able to change and modify the data within the div as he were in word, even he would be able to delete all the information, and in this case the div element will remained just with a br tag, put automatically for not to hide the div element for the user interface.
  • 1:00:00 to 1:30:00: Adding some programming to contenteditable: First creating an HTML lay out of a sort of form with elements h, p and two buttons. Then some sty with CSS, specially to the button elements, having into account that they have interactive styles from default, but, when we apply styles to them, they lose those styles, so it's important to apply again other interactive styles, like hover, clicked, and that kind of pseudoclasses. Juan recommends every element available for react or interact with user should have any kind of style when interacting, or the cursor change to a hand to show that we can click. It's a very important matter of functionality and accesibility. We don't need to exaggarate the styles for hover, a typical use is to add when hover a lighter background-color and a darker border-color, with an outline-style none, like: .btn:hover {background-color: hsl(195, 57%, 90%0;); border-color: hsl(195, 57%, 35%); outline-style: none;}. Then we call the elements with JavaScript:
    • const values = document.querySelectorAll('.value'); // It has all the values that we want user can modify
    • const btnEdit = document.getElementById('btn-edit'); // It has the button edit
    • const btnSave = document.getElementById('btn-save'); // It has the button save
    Then to enable the edition of the elements using the btn-edit we set the following function:
    • btnEdit.addEventListener('click', () => {
    • btnEdit.disabled = true; // To turn on the property disable, making the button edit disabled after modifying.
    • btnSave.disabled = false; // To turn off the property disable, making the button save available to be pressed.
    • values.forEach(element => element.contentEditable = true ); // It parses all the element values for enabling the contenteditable property with the value true, so users can modify the elements value.
    • values[0].focus(); // Put the cursor in the first value for a better functionality.
    If we disable the btnSave we have to be aware that we also have to disable the hover for that button, we can use the pseudoclass selectors :disabled and enabled. For that reason and to fix that we can set the CSS as .btn:enabled:hover {cursor: pointer; background-color: hsl(195, 57%, 90%0;); border-color: hsl(195, 57%, 35%); outline-style: none;}. In the same context we can program the btnSave to be disabled when click and to enable the btnEdit:
    • btnSave.addEventListener('click', () => {
    • btnSave.disabled = true; // To turn on the property disable, making the button edit disabled after modifying.
    • btnEdit.disabled = false; // To turn off the property disable, making the button edit available to be pressed.
    • values.forEach(element => element.contentEditable = false ); // It parses all the element values for disabling the contenteditable property with the value false, so user can not modify the elements value.});
    Turning on one button and turning of another with .disabled = true; and .disabled = false; is frequently used in coding.
  • 1:30:00 to 1:33:00: Also we can code the event input for the container, using: document.querySelector('.customer-container').addEventListener('input', e => {console.log(e.target.innerHTML);}); that way we can show in the console the changes in the container.
  • 1:34:00 to 1:52:00: localStorage. It's the first type of data persistence. According to MDN "The localStorage read-only property of the window interface allows you to access a Storage object for the Document's origin; the stored data is saved across browser sessions." Another type of persistence are cookies and sessionStorage. It belongs to HTML5. If we write console.log(localStorage); we will see in the console the object storage. It's a window property. In very old browser localStorage was not available. Well, localStorage has a serial of methods:
    • localStorage.getItem (key: string) // To call the key stored.
    • localStorage.setItem (key: string, value: string) // To set in the localStorage an item.
    Hence, in the class example, if we put first localStorage.setItem('name', 'value') in the console localStorage.getItem('name'), we're going to receive in return "value". We can find localStorage in DevTools (F12)/Application. It just store strings. In DevTools/Application we can directly delete the stored elements. Any user have his own localStorage.

  • 50:00 to 1:13:00: We can create a builder function for the object 'alumno'. For more information about builder functions: Class - 29. First we create a builder function to make any object 'alumno':
    • function Alumno (nombre, apellido, numeroDeDocumento, telefono, email, detalles) {
    • this.nombre = nombre;
    • this.apellido = apellido;

  • 1:26:00 to 2:40:00: Flexbox. Flexbox is a one-dimensional layout method for arranging items in rows or columns. Items flex (expand) to fill additional space or shrink to fit into smaller spaces. When working with flexbox we first set a container (usually called .flex-container) with the property display: flex. Hence, when display: flex is activated we can add all other properties of flexbox. Here an image of the different examples:
    justify-content
    By default has the value normal: justify-content: normal; but can include the following values for positional alignment:
    • justify-content: center; /* Pack items around the center */
    • justify-content: start; /* Pack items from the start */
    • justify-content: end; /* Pack items from the end */
    • justify-content: flex-start; /* Pack flex items from the start */
    • justify-content: flex-end; /* Pack flex items from the end */
    • justify-content: left; /* Pack items from the left */
    • justify-content: right; /* Pack items from the right */
    And these ones for distributed alignment:
    • justify-content: space-between; /* Distribute items evenly The first item is flush with the start, the last is flush with the end */
    • justify-content: space-around; /* Distribute items evenly Items have a half-size spaceon either end */
    • justify-content: space-evenly; /* Distribute items evenly Items have equal space around them */
    • justify-content: stretch; /* Distribute items evenly Stretch 'auto'-sized items to fit the container */
    Overflow alignment:
    • justify-content: safe center;
    • justify-content: unsafe center;
    Global values
    • justify-content: inherit;
    • justify-content: initial;
    • justify-content: revert;
    • justify-content: revert-layer;
    • justify-content: unset;
    flex-direction
    The flex-direction property sets how flex items are placed in the flex container defining the main axis and the direction (normal or reversed). By default is row.
    • flex-direction: row; /* The direction text is laid out in a line */
    • flex-direction: row-reverse; /* Like row, but reversed */
    • flex-direction: column; /* The direction in which lines of text are stacked */
    • flex-direction: column-reverse; /* Like column, but reversed */
    The following are the global values
    • flex-direction: inherit;
    • flex-direction: initial;
    • flex-direction: revert;
    • flex-direction: revert-layer;
    • flex-direction: unset;
    align-items
    In Flexbox, it controls the alignment of items on the Cross Axis. The following are the basic keywords:
    • align-items: normal;
    • align-items: stretch;
    And this are used for positional alignment, align-items does not take left and right values
    • align-items: center; /* Pack items around the center */
    • align-items: start; /* Pack items from the start */
    • align-items: end; /* Pack items from the end */
    • align-items: flex-start; /* Pack flex items from the start */
    • align-items: flex-end; /* Pack flex items from the end */
    An for baseline alignment
    • align-items: baseline;
    • align-items: first baseline;
    • align-items: last baseline; /* Overflow alignment (for positional alignment only) */
    • align-items: safe center;
    • align-items: unsafe center;
    The following are the global values
    • align-items: inherit;
    • align-items: initial;
    • align-items: revert;
    • align-items: revert-layer;
    • align-items: unset;
    flex-wrap
    The flex-wrap CSS property sets whether flex items are forced onto one line or can wrap onto multiple lines.
    • flex-wrap: nowrap; /* Default value */
    • flex-wrap: wrap;
    • flex-wrap: wrap-reverse;
    These are the global values:
    • flex-wrap: inherit;
    • flex-wrap: initial;
    • flex-wrap: revert;
    • flex-wrap: revert-layer;
    • flex-wrap: unset;
    flex-flow
    The flex-flow CSS shorthand property specifies the direction of a flex container, as well as its wrapping behavior.
    flex-flow: <'flex-direction'>
    • flex-flow: row;
    • flex-flow: row-reverse;
    • flex-flow: column;
    • flex-flow: column-reverse;
    flex-flow: <'flex-wrap'>
    • flex-flow: nowrap;
    • flex-flow: wrap;
    • flex-flow: wrap-reverse;
    flex-flow: <'flex-direction'> and <'flex-wrap'>
    • flex-flow: row nowrap;
    • flex-flow: column wrap;
    • flex-flow: column-reverse wrap-reverse;
    Global values
    • flex-flow: inherit;
    • flex-flow: initial;
    • flex-flow: revert;
    • flex-flow: revert-layer;
    • flex-flow: unset;
    align-content
    The CSS align-content property sets the distribution of space between and around content items along a flexbox's cross-axis or a grid's block axis. Basic positional alignment., align-content does not take left and right values */
    • align-content: center; /* Pack items around the center */
    • align-content: start; /* Pack items from the start */
    • align-content: end; /* Pack items from the end */
    • align-content: flex-start; /* Pack flex items from the start */
    • align-content: flex-end; /* Pack flex items from the end */
    Normal alignment
    • align-content: normal;
    Baseline alignment
    • align-content: baseline;
    • align-content: first baseline;
    • align-content: last baseline;
    Distributed alignment
    • align-content: space-between; /* Distribute items evenly The first item is flush with the start, the last is flush with the end */
    • align-content: space-around; /* Distribute items evenly Items have a half-size space on either end */
    • align-content: space-evenly; /* Distribute items evenly Items have equal space around them */
    • align-content: stretch; /* Distribute items evenly Stretch 'auto'-sized items to fit the container */
    Overflow alignment
    • align-content: safe center;
    • align-content: unsafe center;
    Global values
    • align-content: inherit;
    • align-content: initial;
    • align-content: revert;
    • align-content: revert-layer;
    • align-content: unset;
  • 2:22:00: align-self: it as an align-content for one element, that way we can set the alignment of one element indepently from the others.

Class 40 (Alumni 41) - 10/04/2022. Media, max-width, responsive designing, hierarchy and specificity, list properties, mobile first and BEM nomenclature

Juan's notes Class 40

  • Class - 40: Media
  • Class - 40: max-width
  • Class - 40: responsive
  • Class - 40: Hierarchy and specificity

Timeline and notes Class 40.

  • 00:00 to 31:00: The CSS link has the attribute media that can be set for screen or for print. By default is set in "all" media="all", but it is not showed. There are others as braile, but screen and print are the most used:
    • link rel="stylesheet" href="css/estilos_general.css"
    • link rel="stylesheet" href="css/estilos_pantalla.css" media="screen"
    • link rel="stylesheet" href="css/estilos_impresion.css" media="print"
    Some CSS style using nav:hover, text-decoration for the links, background-color, position the navigation bar with fixed. With media we can set what styles we want to be shown in screens or Print
    • @media screen {h1 {font-style: italic;} h2 {border: 7px solid; padding: .2em;}}
    • @media print {p {text-align: center;}}
  • 31:00 to 36:00: max-width can be used to determine styles at specific device sizes:
    • @media (max-width: 768px) {h1 {background-color: orange; text-decoration: underline;} p {color: olivedrab;}}
    • @media (max-width: 595px) {h1 {background-color: yellow;}
    • @media (max-width: 425px) {p {text-align: left;} h1 {background-color: pink;}}
  • 36:00 to 40:00: We can connect our phone to the PC, for that we have to open the command terminal cmd with Windows + R, also called symbols of system and typing if Windows ipconfig, if Linux ifconfig, enter. Then, almost at the end we will find the real ip address of our device: It says IPv4 with a number like 192.168.100.5. We use that number to open from our cell the LiveServer. We put the modify the first part of the URL with the IPv4, deleting the 127.0.0.1, while we have the same wi-fi network and LiveServer on.
  • 41:00 to 46:00: More about @media (max-width). The order is important because the different styles can overlap others. So is important to order the max-width from the biggest to the smallest. With min-width will be the opposite, from the smallest to the biggest.
  • 46:00 to 58:00: An approach to responsive designing. Using @media we can set some modifications when page dimensions change. For example determining a lower value for margins when max-width: 595px, as follows:
    • @media screen and (max-width: 768px) {h1 {color: red;} body {margin-left: 4em; margin-right: 4em;} .main-wrapper {flex-direction: column;} .publicidad {display: none;}} // The expression screen and (max-width: 768px) is a condition to apply the media changes just in screens at those dimensions. In dimensions lower than 768px the h1 will be red, flex-direction orders the items top to bottom (in a column) and with display we hid the add.
    • @media (max-width: 595px) {body {margin-left: 2em;margin-right: 2em;} section {padding: 0;}} // When dimensions are lower than 595px the body change its margins and sections have no padding.
    We can also decide not to show the adds when print:
    • @media print {.publicidad {display: none;}}
  • 59:00 to 1:42:00: Hierarchy and specificity. Specificity determines which style to apply by the number of selectors of each weight category. For example:
    • section // This selector will apply the styles to all section elements
    • section article p // This selector is more specific than the above so it has more weight and its styles will be applied over the section styles
    In the case of hierarchy. this is the order of hierarchy's selectors: !important > estilos en l칤nea > #id > .clase/pseudo-clase > etiqueta/pseudo-elementos, or in a list:
    1. !important
    2. estilos en l칤nea
    3. #id
    4. .clase/pseudo-clase
    5. etiqueta/pseudo-elementos
    There are some specificity calculator websites, like: https://specificity.keegan.st/
  • 1:43:00 to 2:03:00: Properties of lists. List vignettes are located in the padding zone, but it can be change with list-style-position: inside. We can modigy the properties of lists using li {list-style-type}:
    • list-style-type: disc; // By default
    • list-style-type: none; // Hid the vignettes
    • list-style-type: square;
    • list-style-type: circle;
    • list-style-position: outside; // By default
    • list-style-position: inside;
    • list-style-image: url('img/grande.png'); // To add an image instead of a vignette.
    • .lista5 li {background-image: url('img/grande.png'); background-repeat: no-repeat; background-size: 8px auto; background-position: left 5px center; margin: 5px 0; /* border: 4px solid red; *//* list-style-position: inside; */ padding-left: 15px;} // To add a background-image and located before the element. It's frecuently used.
    • .lista6 {padding: 3em; list-style-position: inside; list-style-image: url('img/star-1.png');} // Another example for adding an image instead of a vignette.
    • .lista6 li:nth-child(2) {list-style-image: url('img/star-2.png');} // Adding the image vignette to nth-child(2), that means the child number two.
    • .lista6 li:nth-child(2):hover {list-style-image: url('img/star-1.png');} // Adding the image vignette to nth-child(2) when hover.
    For ordered lists we can set different vignettes like the following;
    • list-style-type: lower-hebrew; // Vignettes in hebrew characters 넁놩뉑
    • list-style-type: lower-greek; // Vignettes in greek characters 풤쒩풦풧
    • list-style-type: armenian; // Vignettes to armenian characters 쐆쒮얪
    • list-style-type: lower-alpha; // Vignettes in lowercases letters
    • list-style-type: upper-alpha; // Vignettes in uppercases letters
    • list-style-type: lower-roman; // Vignettes in lower roman numbers
    • list-style-type: upper-roman; // Vignettes in upper roman numbers
    • list-style-type: decimal; // By default
    • ol ol {list-style-type: lower-roman;} // To put style in list inside another list.
    List are the conventional form to make navigate menus in websites.
  • 2:03:00 to 2:38:00:Mobile first and the BEM naming convention. Until now we were working with a desktop first approach (from big to small design), but mobile first is from small to big design. It's recommended to use mobile first because nowadays internet is more used from cellphones than any other media. When designing a website is typical to set main with max-width: 1200px;, letting header and footer by default in 100%. In mobile first it's common to order navigate items in columns. When we're working with mobile first designing we use the property min-width instead of max-width used for desktop first. Also its important to set a max-width to the main-nav__list, hence when growing the dimension items stay together and not tend to separate too much. So, some examples for styling a navigation bar using mobile first.
    First we add general styles to the list elements
    • .main-nav {/* border: 4px solid teal; */background: #222;}
    • .main-nav__list {/* border: 4px solid magenta; */ list-style-type: none;display: flex; flex-direction: column; max-width: 1200px; margin: 0 auto;}
    • .main-nav__item {/* border: 4px solid cyan; */text-align: center;flex-grow: 1;}
    • .main-nav__link {/* border: 4px solid red; */ color: #abc; font-weight: bold; padding: .7em; display: block;}
    • .main-nav__link:hover {background-color: #0004; color: #def;}
    Then we set the characteristics for the lower resolutions min-width: 576px
    • @media (min-width: 576px) {main {margin-top: 3em;} .main-nav {position: fixed; top: 0; width: 100%;} .main-nav__list {flex-direction: row;justify-content: space-around;}}
    Then for the a little bigger resolutions min-width: 768px
    • @media (min-width: 768px) {body {color: teal;}main p {text-align: justify;}
    And a little more bigger min-width: 992px
    • @media (min-width: 992px) {.main-nav__list {justify-content: center;}.main-nav__item {flex-grow: 0;}.main-nav__link {padding-left: 2em;padding-right: 2em;}}
    And finally the biggest one min-width: 1200px
    • @media (min-width: 1200px) {.main-nav__list {justify-content: flex-end;}.main-nav__item:last-child .main-nav__link {padding-right: 1em;}.main-nav__link {padding-left: 1em;padding-right: 1em;}}
Go to top.

  • 56:00 to 1:13:00: BEM stands for Block, Element, Modifiers. For more information https://getbem.com/introduction/ and https://getbem.com/naming/:
    • Block is a standalone (in spanish: "ser 칰nico o aut칩nomo") entity that is meaningful on its own. Examples: `header`, `container`, `menu, `checkbox`, `input`.
    • Element is a part of a block that has no standalone meaning and is semantically tied to its block. Examples `menu item`, `list item`, `checkbox caption`, `header title`.
    • Modifier a flag on a block or element. Use them to change appearance or behavior, for the block or its parts. Examples `disabled`, `highlighted`, `checked`, `fixed`, `size big`, `color yellow`
    Website recommended for BEM https://9elements.com/bem-cheat-sheet/. Some generic examples of the use of BEM are:
    • .block {}
    • .block__element1 {}
    • .block__element2 {}
    • .block__element3--xl {}
    • .block__element4--red {}
    • .register-form {}
    • .register-form__complete-name {}
    • .register-form__address {}
    BEM has sense when is used with SASS.

  • 1:41:00 to 2:21:00: Sass is the most mature, stable, and poweful professional grade CSS extension language in the world. It's a CSS preprocessor, and helps us to work easier with CSS. For the record, the CSS processor is the browser. https://sass-lang.com/guide. To apply Sass we can use the conventional and older form called Sass or the new one, easier to work called Sassy CSS or SCSS, the only difference is the use of brackets; for us will be easier to use brackets to work with the VS Code files. With Sass we can organize in an easier and intuitive way the descendant selector, instead of writing nav ul {margin: 0}; nav li {display: inline-block}; we can put everything inside the selector nav, like this: nav { ul {margin: 0} li {display: inline-block}. This way it will be easier to understand the code and work with it. That is called nesting. So, the normal way to work is to divide the CSS files in several parts, so we're going to have a file for the header, another one for the hamburguer and nav, another one for the cards and so on. But, ideally the server should ask just once for this data, so it's better to have just one big file with all the information the browser needs. Sass compile all those files in just a big one, that is called partials. Mixing are like JavaScript functions that let us make groups of CSS declarations that we want to reuse throughout our site, for example: @mixin theme($theme: DarkGray) { background: $theme; box-shadow: 0 0 1px rgba($theme, .25); color: #fff;}, and then we call the theme after, like this: .info {@include theme;} .alert {@include theme($theme: DarkRed);} .success {@include theme($theme: DarkGreen);}

Class 43 (Alumni 44) - 10/13/2022. Installation of Sass.

Timeline and notes Class 43.

  • 00:00 to 57:00: To separate the CSS files with sens we let that work to Sass, creating multiple Sass files, but creating just one CSS file. For that purpose we shall use some patterns as Sass 7-1 pattern https://www.learnhowtoprogram.com/user-interfaces/building-layouts-preprocessors/7-1-sass-architecture. 7 - 1 stands for 7 folder and 1 file. The folders proposed are:
    • abstract // For functions, as the theme example.
    • vendors // For files developed for externals, the developments in here do not depend on us, examples: bootstrap.
    • base // The minimal or fundamentals, as reset (universal styles), typography, normalize.
    • layout // The structure of the site: nav bars, headers, footers, etc.
    • components // Like mini layouts, styles for reusable pieces of the site as buttons, forms, profile pictures.
    • pages // Specific styles for each page.
    • themes // As dark mode or night mode, admin and default themes
    • main.scss
    All these folders inside a folder called sass. This is a good practice suggestion. Other resources are: https://sass-guidelin.es/#architecture, https://gist.github.com/rveitch/84cea9650092119527bc, https://github.com/KittyGiraudel/sass-boilerplate/tree/master/stylesheets. Watch out with the examples because they use @import, and the official documentation of Sass discourages the continued use of the @import rule. Sass will gradually phase it out over the next few years, and eventually remove it from the language entirely. Prefer the @use rule instead. Creating the folders and the files for the 7 - 1 pattern in the integrator project.

  • 20:00 to 58:00: For and while can have no body, it's not common, but possible.
    1. First, we create a function for assigning it to a variable const getRandomNumber = () => Math.round(Math.random()*100); // Math.random creates a random number from 0 to 1 in decimals (0.164523549864, 0.4795486465478), when multiply by 100 they become in numbers between 0 to 100 (16.4523549864, 47.95486465478) and within Math.round they are rounded to a integer between 0 to 100 (16, 48).
    2. Second, we create the variables let number = 93; and let i = 1
    3. Third, we create the for without an initializer and without a counter: for (___; number !== getRandomNumber();___) {console.log(i); i++} // The code will be executed as many times as the random number is different to 93. Outside the for we initialize the i = 1, to watch how many time is executed, we can also pit the initilizer and the counter within the for's parentheses.
    Now, to make a function getRandomBoolean we can do as follows:
    1. First we create a function for assigning it to a variable const getRandomBoolean = () => {const randomNumber = Math.round(Math.random()*100);}
    2. Second we set a conditional if (randomNumber > 50) {return true;} else {return false;} // Half of time it will return true and half of time it will retirn false.
    3. This conditional can be make shorter just putting return randomNumber > 95; // Because randomNumber > 95 will result in true or false.
    When writing a for, if we write a semicolon nothing happens, like this: for (;) {}, but if we put another one: for (;;) {}, it will hang the browser, at least we put for ( ; false ; ) {}. Now, continuing with this wear explanation, a while an have as condition a functions call, like this:
    • let number = 0;
    • while (getRandomBoolean()) {console.log(++number)}
    Or a while without body:

    2. Then we just call the function within the while's condition: while (randomBoolean ()) { /*Without code*/} // In this cases we can delete the brackets, like this: while (randomBoolean)
  • 58:00 to 1:35:00: Synchronic code, or blocking code. Throughout the course we were coding synchronic code. Synchronous, according to MDN, refers to real-time communication where each party receives (and if necessary, processes and replies to) messages instantly (or as near to instantly as possible). A human example is the telephone during a telephone call you tend to respond to another person immediately. Many programming commands are also synchronous for example when you type in a calculation, the environment will return the result to you instantly, unless you program it not to. An example of it is using a for big number in the condition, as 3e9 (that's the same as 3000000000):
    • for (let i = 0; i < 3e9; i++);
    We can also make numbers in JavaScript like this: 45_000 (that's the same as 45000), 45_0_0_0 (45000), 38_000_000 (38000000), it helps for human reading. Now, if we put that for within a function:
    • function delaySync () {for (let i = 0; i < 3e9; i++);} // It will take a while for being executed.
    Then we can call the function delaySync() inside another one, like a serial of steps to simulate a heavy process:
    • function startTasksSync() { // Function start synchoric task
    • console.error('Inicio de tareas');
      • console.warn('>> Inicia tarea A');
      • console.log('>> Tarea A - Paso 1:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea A - Paso 2:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea A - Paso 3:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea A - Paso 4:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea A - Paso 5:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> >> Fin de Tarea A');
    • console.warn('>> Inicia tarea B');
      • console.log('>> Tarea B - Paso 1:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea B - Paso 2:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea B - Paso 3:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea B - Paso 4:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Tarea B - Paso 5:'); delaySync();
      • console.log('>> Fin de Tarea B');
    • console.error('Otras tareas...');}
    The function will be called by the button Start Task (Synchronic Code):
    • document.querySelector('.btn-start-tasks-sync').addEventListener('click', e => {startTasksSync();});
    Now when tasks are executing and we press the button Say Hello, the browser has to wait until it finish the execution to execute the console.log('Hello'). That happens because it is synchronic code, where a task occurs after the other. The problem with this is that browser hang when are executing a task, and that's very bad in a developing process. Now the problemis that the JavaScript engine works with just one thread. Search about web workers API's. So, JavaScript is like a program inside the browser, but not just lives within te browser, it also lives in other devices. In addition to JavaScript the browser also has APIs, additional functionalities for JavaScript inside the browser, they are predetermined to do specific tasks but they're not native JavaScript, but predesign functionalities. In fact, the possibility to access to HTML elements using document.getElementById or document.querySelector, are APIs and as those, browser has a bunch of APIS ready to be used, but inside the browser. In the Command Console or PowerShell we can work with JavaScript, but we won't be able to call document.something because the object document or the object window do not exist. In that environment the API used is node. Hence, some browser's functionalities are document, window, setInterval, setTimeout (the execution of a code in an asynchronic way), or server connection APIs. So in the following example JavaScript engines use the browser API SetTimeout to read the code:
    • setTimeout(() => {console.log(123)}, 2000); // JavaScript give the browser the order to use the API setTimeout to count two seconds, while it keeps reading the code. That's why in the console this part of the code is showed at the end.
    • console.log(100);
    • console.log(200);
    • console.log(300);
    • console.log(400);
    • console.log(500);
    While the browser setTimeout API is counting we can execute other things with JavaScript and it will work without problem, like pressing say Hello, because setTimeout is no blocking code, that is asynchronic code.
  • 1:28:00 to 1:41:00: Asynchronic code, or no blocking code. For this coding the perfect example is setTimeout, this instruction is asynchronic, but the code within that shall be executed when time ends is synchronic code. Example of this can be seen in Class - 44: Synchronic vs asynchronic code. So the only way to do asynchronic code is through APIs, setTimeout is one of them, but there are others very important to communicate with the server (e.g promises).
  • 1:42:00 to 2:03:00: When we program setTimeout in 0 seconds it will be executed after the other lines of code, because as it is explain above JavaScript orders the browser to count while it keeps reading. So, after reading, it will execute the code inside the setTimeout. So, that's a trick to execute some tasks in the order we want. But, when doing this, setting a code to be executed after another, using setTimeout and functions, we have to be careful not to make a callback hell, also called pyramid of doom.
    Call back hell. Example 1
    Call back hell. Example 2
  • 2:04:00 to 2:24:00: AJAX - 1. AJAX stands for Asynchronous JavaScript and XML. According to MDN AJAX is not a technology in itself, but rather an approach to using a number of existing technologies together, including HTML or XHTML, CSS, JavaScript, DOM, XML, XSLT, and most importantly the XMLHttpRequest object. With AJAX we can ask for updates to the server without the need to update the entire page, that's how functions the likes in social media platforms, also when we have diferent pages in a web site, we can program JavaScript with AJAX to charge certain information of texts, colors and themes, using AJAX, so the user change from page to page without reloading. That's a way to work. We can see how AJAX works in Network, putting throtting 3G Fast to simulate an slower connection and watching the responses to any patition done to the server (Preview shows details of the response).
  • 2:24:00 to 2:39:00: How does AJAX works. weel, with the XMLHttpRequest object. First we create a variable with the function XMLHttpRequest(), like this: const xhr = new XMLHttpRequest() // the name xhr (stands for XMLHttpRequest) is a common way to call this variable, another used name is req (stands for request). It is necessary to write the upper and lowercases as same as it is. And the word new is also necessary. According to MDN XMLHttpRequest (XHR) objects are used to interact with servers. You can retrieve data from a URL without having to do a full page refresh. This enables a Web page to update just part of a page without disrupting what the user is doing. XMLHttpRequest is used heavily in AJAX programming. Despite its name, XMLHttpRequest can be used to retrieve any type of data, not just XML. Well the new object assign to the variable const xhr will have the property xhr.readyState and it has the following values, that means an XHR client exists in one of the following states:
    • 0: Instance created // UNSENT. Client has been created. open() not called yet.
    • 1: Instance setted // OPENED. open() has been called.
    • 2: Sharing of headers between client and server // HEADERS_RECEIVED. send() has been called, and headers and status are available.
    • 3: Transfer of information // LOADING. Downloading; responseText holds partial data.
    • 4: End of communication (by succes or failure) // DONE. The operation is complete.
    According to MDN the XMLHttpRequest.readyState property returns the state an XMLHttpRequest client is in. When we create the const xhr = new XMLHttpRequest() we instance create the instance. If we code xhr.open('get', 'texto.txt'), that sets the conection method and the resource we're demainding to the server, and xhr.readyState turns into the value 1. Examples of methods are in forms, due they also have the property method with the possible values 'post' (used for uploading data) or 'get' (used for searching data), there are another less used methods. The method 'get' will be always used for the resource xhr.open 'texto.txt'. When we code xhr.send() JavaScript send the order to the browser and continues reading the code. xhr.send() is asynchronic code. When we check xhr.readyState after coding xhr.send() the console will show a value of 4, that means the operation is complete, passing from 1 to 4 because is asynchronic code. To see the response we can use xhr.responseText, that will show the content of the response. We can set the time to show the response in the order we want, like this:
    • setTimeout(() => {console.log('xhr.readyState:', xhr.readyState); // 4
    • console.log('xhr.responseText:', xhr.responseText);}, 5000); // 'Hola mundo!'
  • 2:40:00 to 2:41:00: Practice: Do the same thing Juan did but with a button. When clicking the button to ask for the resource, can be an .txt file and program a setTimeout to show the response in the console. Farewell. If that works use .innerHTML to show it in a div or H1.     • 'This is a text'.includes('tex') // True

    So we make a conditional if the contentType does not include text/plain we will do something, like saying the content received is not the content-type expected. The nature and format of a document are known as MIME types, according to MDN https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Basics_of_HTTP/MIME_types, a media type (also known as a Multipurpose Internet Mail Extensions or MIME type) indicates the nature and format of a document, file, or assortment of bytes. To know the entire list of MIME Types, visit: https://www.iana.org/assignments/media-types/media-types.xhtml. Warning: Browsers use the MIME type, not the file extension, to determine how to process a URL, so it's important that web servers send the correct MIME type in the response's Content-Type header. If this is not correctly configured, browsers are likely to misinterpret the contents of files, sites will not work correctly, and downloaded files may be mishandled.

    To understand better the 'nulish coalescing' operator: if we have a variable a = null if the code a ?? 2 the answer will be 2, that means if a is null return 2. Also works with several other statements, as a ?? b ?? null ?? 2 ?? undefined (will return 2, the firts one different from null or undefinded). This operator is only available in ES11 and posterior.

    In summary, both are forms to send data, but JSON is very similar to a JavaScript object, you can put in JSON any type of JavaScript value, except by undefined. This undefined is a value we just find in JavaScript. JSON is very useful and very used with AJAX.

So, what happens is that the system receives the text in form of JSON format or XML format and translate the data into objects with live and sens.

  • 32:00 to 45:00: Exceptions - Try/Catch. An exception is an error object that has been thrown. To understand better this we should know the type of errors in JavaScript. Source A definitve guide to handling errors in JavaScript:
    1. Uncaught Range Error. This error occurs in Google Chrome under a few various scenarios. Another reason why this error happens is if you have passed a value that is out of a function's parameter's range.
    2. Uncaught TypeError: Cannot set property. This error occurs when you set a property on an undefined reference. For example initializing a reference without a value and try to accessing its properties.
    3. Uncaught TypeError: Cannot read property. This is one of the most frequently occurring errors in JavaScript. This error occurs when you attempt to read a property or call a function on an undefined object. An undefined object is one of the many possible causes of this error. Another prominent cause of this issue can be an improper initialization of the state while rendering the UI. The essence to avoid this here is to always initialize your variables properly before using them.
    4. TypeError: 'undefined' is not an object. This error occurs in Safari when you try to access the properties of or call a method on an undefined object. The solution to this error is also the same make sure that you have initialized your variables correctly and they are not undefined when a property or method is accessed.
    5. TypeError: null is not an object. This is, again, similar to the previous error. It occurs on Safari, and the only difference between the two errors is that this one is thrown when the object whose property or method is being accessed is null instead of undefined. Since null is a value explicitly set to a variable and not assigned automatically by JavaScript. This error can occur only if you're trying to access a variable you set null by yourself. So, you need to revisit your code and check if the logic that you wrote is correct or not.
    6. TypeError: Cannot read property 'length'. This error occurs in Chrome when you try to read the length of a null or undefined object. The cause of this issue is similar to the previous issues, but it occurs quite frequently while handling lists; hence it deserves a special mention. However, in the newer versions of Chrome, this error is reported as Uncaught TypeError: Cannot read properties of undefined. The fix, again, is to ensure that the object whose length you're trying to access exists and is not set to null.
    7. TypeError: 'undefined' is not a function. This error occurs when you try to invoke a method that doesn't exist in your script, or it does but can not be referenced in the calling context. This error usually occurs in Google Chrome, and you can solve it by checking the line of code throwing the error. If you find a typo, fix it and check if it solves your issue. If you have used the self-referencing keyword this in your code, this error might arise if this is not appropriately bound to your context. To solve this, you must pass the proper reference to the function by binding it with the bind() method.
    8. ReferenceError: event is not defined. This error occurs when you try to access a reference not defined in the calling scope. This usually happens when handling events since they often provide you with a reference called event in the callback function. This error can occur if you forget to define the event argument in your function's parameters or misspell it. This error might not occur in Internet Explorer or Google Chrome (as IE offers a global event variable and Chrome attaches the event variable automatically to the handler), but it can occur in Firefox. So it's advisable to keep an eye out for such small mistakes.
    9. TypeError: Assignment to constant variable. This is an error that arises out of carelessness. If you try to assign a new value to a constant variable, you'll be met with such a result. While it seems easy to fix right now, imagine hundreds of such variable declarations and one of them mistakenly defined as const instead of let! Unlike other scripting languages like PHP, there's minimal difference between the style of declaring constants and variables in JavaScript. Therefore it's advisable to check your declarations first of all when you face this error. You could also run into this error if you forget that the said reference is a constant and use it as a variable. This indicates either carelessness or a flaw in your app's logic. Make sure to check this when trying to fix this issue.
    10. (unknown): Script error. A script error occurs when a third-party script sends an error to your browser. This error is followed by (unknown) because the third-party script belongs to a different domain than your app. The browser hides other details to prevent leaking sensitive information from the third-party script.You can not resolve this error without knowing the complete details. Here's what you can do to get more information about the error:
      1. Add the crossorigin attribute in the script tag.
      2. Set the correct Access-Control-Allow-Origin header on the server hosting the script.
      3. [Optional] If you don't have access to the server hosting the script, you can consider using a proxy to relay your request to the server and back to the client with the correct headers.
      Once you can access the details of the error, you can then set down to fix the issue, which will probably be with either the third-party library or the network.
    When an error is produced it throws an exception. So, it's important to catch the error to allow the JS engine to continue working and reading other parts of the code. So, we catch the exceptions using the words try and catch, like this:
    If we have code inside of a try block and an error occurs the engine will go to catch en will execute the code within it. In the variable error from the catch we're going to have the details about the error., that means the object error, remember an error is an object. In catch we can set or customize a message as console.log('Ups!, something went wrong!') and execute a function as showErrorMessage(); or change the value of a variable, like i = 0; or assign an unknown value to the variable user: user = 'unknown';. After the catch we can continue reading the following code without problem, despite the error and the exceptions.
  • 46:00 to 51:00: JavaScript errors have three properties:
    1. message: A string value that carries the error message
    2. name: The type of error that occurred (We'll dive deep into this in the next section)
    3. stack: The stack trace of the code executed when the error occurred.
    The idea is not to put trys and catchs all over the code, but in those places where we know errors can occur and it doesn't depend on us. Because if there are syntax errors the code won't work directly with or without try and catch.

  • 1:58:00 to 2:13:00: JavaScript Strict Mode. According to MDN "JavaScript's strict mode is a way to opt in to a restricted variant of JavaScript, thereby implicitly opting-out of "sloppy mode". Strict mode isn't just a subset: it intentionally has different semantics from normal code. Browsers not supporting strict mode will run strict mode code with different behavior from browsers that do, so don't rely on strict mode without feature-testing for support for the relevant aspects of strict mode. Strict mode code and non-strict mode code can coexist, so scripts can opt into strict mode incrementally Strict mode makes several changes to normal JavaScript semantics":
    • Eliminates some JavaScript silent errors by changing them to throw errors.
    • Fixes mistakes that make it difficult for JavaScript engines to perform optimizations: strict mode code can sometimes be made to run faster than identical code that's not strict mode.
    • Prohibits some syntax likely to be defined in future versions of ECMAScript.
     JavaScript Strict Mode was added in ECMAScript 5 in 2009. To define it we set the string 'use strict' in the .js file, inside a script label or within the scope of a function.
    • With Strict Mode we can not assign variables without using the key words var, let or const.
    • With Strict Mode when assigning a value to a primitive value, as undefined = 3; it will throw a TypeError object. // this is also not possible in sloopy mode, but in strict mode throws an error.
    • With Strict Mode when creating a property to a boolean value, as true.a = 3; it will throw a TypeError object. /// this is also not possible in sloopy mode, but in strict mode throws an error.
    • Some words as public is reserved in Stric Mode, so we can't create a variable with that word. Other reserved words are while, for, this etc.

This logic can be used to make the fibonacci secuence with JavaScript. JavaScripts works with just one execution thread, so when is busy can't do something else, is like a very basic guy.

    The problem with this style of coding is that it creates the known and famous Pyramid of Doom or Hell of Callbacks.   • 2:51:00 to 3:35:00: Promises: According to MDN "The Promise object represents the eventual completion (or failure) of an asynchronous operation and its resulting value. A Promise is a proxy for a value not necessarily known when the promise is created. It allows you to associate handlers with an asynchronous action's eventual success value or failure reason. This lets asynchronous methods return values like synchronous methods: instead of immediately returning the final value, the asynchronous method returns a promise to supply the value at some point in the future." Promises can have the following states:
    • pending: initial state, neither fulfilled nor rejected.
    • fulfilled: meaning that the operation was completed successfully.
    • rejected: meaning that the operation failed.
     An example of a promise in JavaScript is when we purchase something in a fast food restaurant and the restaurant gives us a black disc or a card with a sensor that notifies when the order is ready. So the disc is the promise that you will receive or maybe not your food ordered. Some abstract and not to effective examples to understand promises. If we don't use AJAX or asynchronous code the use of promises has no purpose. To execute something when the promise is fullfilled we use the method of promises .then(). To create a promise we use the operator new and set the Callbacks resolve and reject:
    • const promise = new Promise((resolve, reject) => {}); // the Callbacks resolve and reject are standard ways to call those Callbacks.
    So what Juan does is to put the new promise within the function getPostPromise() and to return the new Promise((resolve, reject) =>{}); that means the result of the promise. We can set different rejects and resolves within the function, but inside the Callback resolve() we have to put the object that we want to put in the method .then. For the example is the post: resolve(post);. Next example of the use of then to receive sequential responses and make differente requests without creating a Pyramid of Doom:
    We put a .catch at the end of the code if something is wrong: .catch(error) => {console.log(error);}. Hence any possible error when calling the promises will be catched. It will work as a try/catch statement. A brief summary and some announcements about the next class topic, basically the use of async/await syntax. Promises came with ECMAScript 6, it solved the Pyramid of Doom problem, but it's a little bit complex though, so some years later it was added the async/await syntax.

  • 48:00 to 1:27:00: Async/await syntax, according to MDN "The async function declaration declares an async function where the await keyword is permitted within the function body. The async and await keywords enable asynchronous, promise-based behavior to be written in a cleaner style, avoiding the need to explicitly configure promise chains." We are working with the file Class - 48: AJAX nested 2. The following is an example of the use of async and await:
    The keyword 'await' is used to put the promise's response in the variable determined, for example:
    • let response = await getPostPromise(1); // Whatever the response is (what we set in the resolve), when the promise is resolved, await will assign it to variable let response.
    The keyword 'await' is only valid in async functions. So, function using 'await' have to be determined as async, like this:
    • async function getPostsPromiseAsyncAwait() {console.log('Start of requests'); let response = await getPostPromise(1); console.log('End of requests');} // If we don't use the word wait the variable response will have the promise, probably with the status "pending", not the response.
    Therefore, when we use await the JavaScript engine literally awaits within the function for the promise to be solved, but the engine keeps reading the following code outside the function. When the function is async works as any other function, but allows us to use the term await within it. When we have to many awaits and with one response we call the next one, we can use a for, inside a try, like this:
    Finally a fictional example:
    • async function getPostsPromiseAsyncAwait() {
    • const id = 100;
    • const persona = await getPersona(id);
    • const cursos = await getCursos(persona.dni);
    • cursos[0].confirmado = true;
    • cursos[1].confirmado = false;
    • const respuesta = await confirmarCursos(cursos);
    • console.log(respuesta);}

  • 1:27:00 to 1:31:00:AJAX summary. AJAX is asynchronous communication, we can do it by XMLHttpRequest or by fetch that uses promises. Generally we don't create promises from zero, what professionals do is to use already existin functions or libraries that has promises incorporated. Promises give us an object that represents an operation with a unknown result, and that result can be resolved or reject. For promises, if we don't use async/await we have to use, instead, the methods .then and catch. The mnethod then obtains the resolve from the promise and catch receives the error. Meanwhile, with async/await we put the term await before the promises, being the result of a function or not and await for the result not the promise itself. When we get the result from the promise we can call the next one, even using a for. Asynchronous code is not always used to access to remote resources, we can also use it for local resources. Some browser API's can allow us to work with promises. For example the reading of fisical files can be done through promises. That means we ask the browser to read a file and meanwhile we can be doing something else in our code. Another example is when we want to access to the webcam or to some other devices, they can also work using promises.

  • 48:00 to 56:00: Node.js installation. Explanation about semantic versioning: Given a version number MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH, increment the:
    • MAJOR version when you make incompatible API changes
    • MINOR version when you add functionality in a backwards compatible manner
    • PATCH version when you make backwards compatible bug fixes
    Additional labels for pre-release and build metadata are available as extensions to the MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH format. For more information check https://semver.org/. FOr example La 칰ltima versi칩n con LTS long term support es 18.12.1 LTS18 (Versi칩n mayor, se modifica cuando las modificaciones generaron alguna incompatibilidad con algo). 12 (Versi칩n menor, se cambia cuando se agreg칩 una nueva caracter칤stica). 1 (parche, cuando se hace una correcci칩n). The version LTS offers a Long Term Support (30 months).

    We initialize with const http = require('http'); then set the callback inside the .createServer method with the arguments request and response, set the status, set the header and the response with res.writeHead(status, headers), res.write('We can put here parts of the response') and res.end('Response's body'). The low port numbers like 21, 22, 80, 443 are standard number for specific functions or utilities. Other bigger port number are not available to use, like 2200, 22005. Let's remember a server is a software installed in some device. In a same device we can have several servers installed, for example a web server, a fs server and a ftp server. From the outside the request will achieve the device, so the way we have to link the request to the specific server is using a determined port number. Usually, when developing, we use the port 8080, as we can see in the image and we stablish with the method .listen what to do when the connection is succeded or what to do if event 'error' happens with method .on (In Node.js the method .on is equivalent to addEventListener for events when working with JavaScript in the browser). If 8080 is in use, we can chage for 8081, 8082, 8083 and so on. The number port number 3000 is also quite used. But this not a standard, is just a habit from Node.js programmers. We create a const in uppercase to indicate in something that won't be modify or won't never change in the project. When we have this code setted and type in the Terminal node ., it will show in the console HTTP Server listening in the port 8080.

    • const method = req.method;
    • const url = req.url;
    Second, by using a multiple variables declaration form:
    • const {method, url} = req;
